<article id="post-14" class="post-14 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-1-01 tag-gordon tag-helen tag-jamie tag-lillian tag-snake-charmer tag-sy tag-thom">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<div class="entry-meta">
			<a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/" title="12:01 am" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date" datetime="2014-12-24T00:01:00+00:00" pubdate>December 24, 2014</time></a><span class="byline"> by <span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/author/wildbow/" title="View all posts by wildbow" rel="author">wildbow</a></span></span>		</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		<h1 class="entry-title">Taking Root 1.1</h1>	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content">
		<p><span style="color:#ffffff;">.</span>                                                                                                                                                      <strong><a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2015/03/14/taking-root-1-2/">Next</a></strong></p>
<p><em>How does it go?  The first lesson, something even the uninitiated know.  For life to flourish on the most basic level, it requires four elements.  Carbon, oxygen, nitrogen and hydrogen.</em></p>
<p>We were doing fine on that count.  The air around us was stale, but it was still oxygen.  Water ran around and below us, flowing over our bare feet, redirected from gutters to the building&#8217;s inside.</p>
<p>What had once been a barn had been made into a warehouse, then abandoned partway through a third set of changes.  A floor of old wooden slats reached only halfway down the length of the old building, what had once been a hayloft.  If we stood on the edge, we could look down at the floor below to see uneven floorboards on top of compacted dirt.  The original barn&#8217;s door was still there, mounted on rollers.  I leaned over to get a better look.  I could see a table, some scattered papers, books, and a blackboard.  The only light was that which came in through windows.  A scattered set placed on the upper floor, and more well above head height on the lower one.</p>
<p>Aside from the four of us, one other thing occupied the hayloft.  It was hard to make out in the dim light that filtered in through the window, like an eel in dark water, and if it weren&#8217;t for the fact that we&#8217;d seen it approach, we might not have noticed it at all.  Sleek, four-legged, and tall enough I couldn&#8217;t have reached its shoulder if I stood on my toes, it was wound around the pillar as a snake might be.  Unlike a snake, though, it had four long limbs, each with four long digits, tipped with claws.  Head flowed into neck, which flowed into shoulder and body without a without prominent ridge, bump, bone or muscle to interrupt the sequence.</p>
<p>It uncoiled, setting a claw on the floor, and the old floorboards didn&#8217;t elicit an audible creak.  Large as it was, it managed to distribute weight too evenly, and used its tail to suspend some of its weight.</p>
<p>It didn&#8217;t walk, but <em>slinked</em>, each foot falling in front of the last as it passed within three feet of us.  Its wide mouth parted, showing just a hint of narrow white teeth.</p>
<p>There was no cover, nothing to hide us from it.</p>
<p>I saw its nostrils flare.  It opened its mouth to taste the air with a flick of a thin tongue.</p>
<p>The way things looked, we were very close to doing the opposite of &#8216;flourishing&#8217;.</p>
<p>It was hard to put into words, but my thoughts connected with that thought, and it was <em>funny.</em></p>
<p>I grinned, and flakes of wax fell from my face at the movement.  I watched the thing continue onward, toward the back of the hayloft, head turning as it sniffed the surroundings.  It unwound its long tail from the wooden pillar that held up the one end of the overhanging hayloft, and it moved with a slow carefulness.</p>
<p>I stared at its eye, and saw how it didn&#8217;t move as the head swept from one side to the next, the slit of the iris barely changing in response as the faint light from the window swept over its head.</p>
<p>&#8220;It&#8217;s blind,&#8221; I whispered.</p>
<p>The movements of the creature came to a halt.  It froze, nostrils wide.</p>
<p>Gordon, just to my left, put out a hand, covering my mouth.  He was tense, lines on his neck standing out.  Trying to put on a brave face, as our leader.  Gordon, strong, handsome, likeable, talented.  A veneer covered his face, as it did all of us, almost clear, cracked and white at the corners of his lips where he&#8217;d changed his expression, coming away in flakes at his hairline, where his hair was covered by the same substance.</p>
<p>The creature turned, and as it did its tail moved around until it touched the outside edge of the makeshift gutter that we were all standing in, fine emerald scales rasping against wood.</p>
<p>When Gordon whispered his response, I could barely hear him utter, &#8220;It&#8217;s not<em> deaf</em>.&#8221;</p>
<p>I nodded, and he pulled his hand away.</p>
<p>I had a glimpse of the girls.  Helen and Lillian.  As different as night and day.  Lillian was bent over, hood up and over her head, hiding her face, hands clutching the straps of her bag, white knuckled.  Terrified, and rightly so.  The coating on her face was flaking badly.</p>
<p>In contrast, Helen&#8217;s face didn&#8217;t betray a flicker of emotion.  Her golden hair, normally well cared for, cultivated into tight rolls, was damp and falling out of place.  Water ran down her face, splashing in through the side of the window where the makeshift gutter came in, and the droplets didn&#8217;t provoke one flinch or batted eyelash.  She could have been a statue, and she&#8217;d kept her face still enough that the wax that covered it hadn&#8217;t broken, which only helped the effect.</p>
<p>Still and silent, we watched as the creature moved to the far corner of the hayloft.</p>
<p>It snapped, and the four curved fangs were the only ones that were any wider than a pencil, visible for only an instant before the head disappeared into detritus piled in the corner.  A furred form struggled before the creature could raise its head.  No swallowing, per se.  Gravity did the work, as teeth parted and the prey fell down its long throat.</p>
<p>A second bite let it collect another, small and young enough it couldn&#8217;t even struggle.  Tiny morsels.</p>
<p>&#8220;Kitties,&#8221; Lillian whispered, horror overtaking fear in her expression.</p>
<p><em>Mama kitty shouldn&#8217;t have had her babies in the same building as the monster,</em> I thought.  <em>Wallace&#8217;s law at work</em>.</p>
<p>Gordon nudged me.  He pointed.</p>
<p>The window.</p>
<p>I nodded.</p>
<p>The makeshift gutter was little more than a <em>trough</em>, with little care given for the leaks here and there, and it fed into wooden barrels at the edge of the upper floor, with more channels and troughs leading into sub-chambers and tanks below.  It had been running long enough for debris and grime to accumulate, a combination of silt and scum collecting at the very bottom to make it treacherous.  Our progress was slow, and I had to remind myself that anything faster threatened to make <em>noise</em>, or risked a fall.</p>
<p>As if to follow the thought, Lillian&#8217;s foot skidded on the bottom of the trough, and she tipped forward, straight into Helen&#8217;s arms.  The creature stopped its slow consumption of the cat&#8217;s litter.</p>
<p>We were frozen, waiting, while the creature sniffed the air.</p>
<p>It returned to its meal.</p>
<p>We made our way out, everyone but me flipping up their hoods to ward off the rain.  I let the droplets fall where they would, on hair that refused to be bound down beneath a thick layer of waterproofing wax.</p>
<p>There was no ledge outside the window, only the real gutter.  Bigger and more solid, if still treacherous with seasons of accumulated grime.  The roof loomed above us, more up than over, as barn roofs were wont to be.  Red leaves collected here and there.</p>
<p>&#8220;I stay,&#8221; Helen murmured.</p>
<p>There was no questioning it, no argument.  We couldn&#8217;t afford to make the noise, and it made a degree of sense.</p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;ll go first,&#8221; I volunteered, craning my head a bit to see the way down.  Being the sort of building it was, the barn-turned-warehouse-turned-something-else was tall, with a long way to the bottom.  The gutter pointed groundward at the corner, fixed to the brick exterior at regular points by lengths of metal.  It worked as a ladder, but not one that was fun to use.  The &#8216;rungs&#8217; were too far apart, too close to the wall.</p>
<p>Someone grabbed my arm.  I thought it would be Gordon or Helen, as they had the personalities to be arm-grabbers.  It wasn&#8217;t.</p>
<p>&#8220;You go <em>second</em>,&#8221; Lillian whispered to me.  &#8220;I know you well enough to know that If you go before me you&#8217;ll look up my skirt.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Me?&#8221; I tried to sound innocent.</p>
<p>Gordon jabbed me.  His expression was no-nonsense, his green eyes a steely grey beneath his hood, absorbing the colors of the clouds above.  His mouth was a grim line.</p>
<p>&#8220;Okay,&#8221; I conceded.</p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;ll take your bag,&#8221; Gordon whispered.  Again, there was no argument.  Lillian handed over the backpack, loaded down with tools and supplies.</p>
<p>She accepted Gordon&#8217;s support in getting down to the downspout, and began her slow descent.</p>
<p>I fidgeted.  My eye traveled over our surroundings, buildings scattered like they&#8217;d been blown around by strong winds and planted where they lay.  Older structures had a charm to them, simplicity and a character that came with age and gentle wear and tear.  The oldest and the newest buildings had been shored up by strategic plant growth, branches weaving into and through damaged sections, growing to complement masonry, around bricks and supports.  The very newest growths had a characteristic red tint to the leaves.  The rest were dead, left to petrify.</p>
<p>The Academy loomed above it all, those same elements taken to an extreme.  It had been an old collection of buildings once.  A rush to grow and meet surging demand had led to a lot of the same haphazard growth.</p>
<p>It all had an <em>odor</em>.  There were smells that became second nature, and there were smells that were ingrained in the psyche as <em>bad</em> smells.  Ones that spoke of death, of long sickness, and of violence.  Rendered fat, decay, and blood.  Each were heavy on the air.</p>
<p>Ironic, that things so overgrown and reeking of decay were the parts of the city charged with progress.</p>
<p><em>You&#8217;d think the rain would wash away the smell.</em></p>
<p>I checked.  Lillian had moved down one rung.  I shifted my weight from one foot to the next, annoyed.</p>
<p>She wasn&#8217;t one of us.  She was new.  Allowances had to be made.</p>
<p>It wasn&#8217;t the first time I had told myself any of those things.  I&#8217;d heard it from Gordon.  It didn&#8217;t make it any less annoying.</p>
<p>I bent down, peering over the edge of the gutter to the road below.  I could see the windows, the boxes further down.</p>
<p>&#8220;Sy,&#8221; Gordon hissed the words, &#8220;What are you doing?&#8221;</p>
<p>Gripping the ledge, I swung myself over.</p>
<p>I let go, and enjoyed both the moment of utter terror and Lillian&#8217;s gasp of horror, before my fingers caught hold of the window frame below.</p>
<p>My right foot slipped on the damp windowsill, scraping peeling paint off and away before I brought it back up to the sill.  Water and paint flakes sprayed below.</p>
<p>When I looked up, Gordon&#8217;s head was poking over the edge, looking down at me.</p>
<p>He moved his head, and I could hear him speak, very patiently, to Lillian, &#8220;Keep going.  Don&#8217;t mind him.&#8221;</p>
<p>Peering in the window, I could see the interior, the lower floor.  The desk, the notes on the experiment.  Another table was heavy with lines of bottles, vials, jugs, and yet more papers, scattered.  Rain poured down on me, tracing its way down the back of my neck, beneath my shirt.  The waxed and waterproof cowl and short cloak had kept my shirt dry, and I shivered at the sensation.</p>
<p>I tested the window, and was utterly surprised to find it latched.  I drew a key from my pocket, trying to fit it into the gap, hoping to lift the latch, but it proved too thick.</p>
<p>The key went back in place.  I removed my hands from the windowsill one at a time, to dry them in my armpits and then reposition my grip.</p>
<p>Gripping the windowsill, I strained my body, reaching down and to the right.  The doorframe that bounded the large sliding door was just out of reach&#8230;</p>
<p>Holding the windowsill with my left hand, reaching with my right leg, I touched the frame with my big toe.  I found a grip, and I used it to better position myself.  Fingers dug into the space between bricks, where water had worn away mortar, and I heaved myself over, using my toes and only my toes to perch on the top of the doorway.</p>
<p>Were it any other door, I wouldn&#8217;t have fussed, but I was still just high enough off the ground to have cause to worry.  This had been a barn, and this door was the type that let wagons or draft horses inside.</p>
<p>I paused on top of the door, cleaning my hands of wet and grit.</p>
<p>&#8220;Watching you do that is making me nervous,&#8221; Lillian said, looking down at me.  She&#8217;d progressed two more &#8216;rungs&#8217;.  She was the shortest of us, next to me, it didn&#8217;t make it easier for her.</p>
<p>I flashed her a grin, and more of the waterproofing wax that I&#8217;d caked onto my face cracked.</p>
<p>I worked my way down to a crouch, still on top of the door, then slid down, draping my front against the door itself.  I let myself drop the rest of the way, landing bare-footed in mud.</p>
<p>I couldn&#8217;t get the smile off my face as I passed beneath the drain pipe, making a point of looking up at Lillian, who was making a point of her own in turn, glaring down at me, very clearly annoyed.</p>
<p>&#8220;You had an audience,&#8221; a soft voice stated.</p>
<p>I turned.</p>
<p>Amid empty crates and a door that had been taken off its hinges, jumbled together as trash and detritus, I could make out the fifth member of our contingent.  Jamie had a book in his lap, our collected boots and shoes neatly organized around him, and he had company.  A black-skinned boy with a hood and cloak far too large for him, tattered enough that it had probably been a hand-me-down for the last person to own it.  His eyes were wide.</p>
<p>&#8220;I thought you were keeping lookout,&#8221; I said.</p>
<p>&#8220;I was.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;The whole point of being lookout is that you tell us if there&#8217;s trouble.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Is he trouble?&#8221;  Jamie asked.</p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;m no trouble,&#8221; the boy&#8217;s words flowed right off the back of Jamie&#8217;s, without a heartbeat of hesitation.  &#8220;The trouble is inside.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;The snake thing,&#8221; I said.</p>
<p>&#8220;You saw it?&#8221; he asked.  His eyes went wider.  &#8220;Then you should know if you&#8217;re going to steal something, you shouldn&#8217;t steal from there.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;We&#8217;re not stealing,&#8221; I said.  &#8220;We&#8217;re just looking.&#8221;</p>
<p>The boy didn&#8217;t respond.  He watched Lillian&#8217;s glacially slow descent.</p>
<p>I met Jamie&#8217;s eyes.  If it weren&#8217;t for Helen, who was a special case, I might have called Jamie the quiet one.  He wore eyeglasses, though there were all sorts of ways to fix or replace bad eyes, and his hair was long beneath his hood.  Not out of any style or affectation.  He simply never liked how it looked when it was short.  His face was narrow, his eyes large as he shifted his gaze to look from me to Lillian.  His hands held firm to a book that sat across his knees.</p>
<p>&#8220;Helen?&#8221; he asked.</p>
<p>&#8220;Stayed upstairs.&#8221;</p>
<p>A nod.</p>
<p>I wanted him to figure out how to deal with our bystander, given how he&#8217;d failed to warn us about the boy in the first place, but Jamie was silent.</p>
<p>&#8220;What&#8217;s your name?&#8221; I asked.</p>
<p>&#8220;Mine?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;I know <em>his</em>,&#8221; I said, striving to not sound as annoyed at the question as I <em>felt</em>.  I pointed at Jamie to make myself as clear as possible.</p>
<p>&#8220;Thomas.  My friends call me Thom.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Did you hear about the crying man of Butcher&#8217;s Row?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Sly,&#8221; Jamie said, suddenly paying attention to the issue.  The name was a warning.</p>
<p>But Thom gave an answer, &#8220;That stitched that went crazy.  Remembered things.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;That&#8217;s the one.  Do you remember Mother Hen?&#8221;</p>
<p>Thom nodded.  &#8220;That nurse who- the babies.&#8221;</p>
<p>He looked rather uneasy now.</p>
<p>&#8220;That&#8217;s right,&#8221; I said, doing my best to sound calm, reassuring.  &#8220;The nurse.  <em>Yes</em>.  Both got caught, right?  Everything got tied up neatly?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Yeah,&#8221; Thom said.  He couldn&#8217;t meet my eyes, so he focused on Lillian instead.  &#8220;The authorities from the Academy got them.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Exactly, Thom,&#8221; I said, &#8220;But who told the authorities?&#8221;</p>
<p>His eyes moved.  To me, then Jamie, to Lillian, and then the barn-turned-warehouse.</p>
<p>I was nodding before the word came out of his mouth.  &#8220;You.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;You&#8217;re clever,&#8221; I praised him.</p>
<p>&#8220;Why?&#8221;</p>
<p>I made the universal gesture for money, rubbing thumb against two fingers.</p>
<p>&#8220;Really?&#8221;</p>
<p>I nodded.</p>
<p>The gears were shifting in his head.  Processing, calculating.</p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;ve heard things,&#8221; he said.</p>
<p>&#8220;I bet.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Useful things.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;I don&#8217;t doubt it,&#8221; I said.</p>
<p>&#8220;I can get money for it?  For telling people?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;If you know who to tell, and how to sell it,&#8221; I said.</p>
<p>His expression changed, a frown.  Disappointment.</p>
<p>Tick, tick, turn turn.  The gears in his head were still moving.</p>
<p>He wasn&#8217;t dumb, even if he wasn&#8217;t much of an actor.  Then again, he was only ten or so.</p>
<p>I could guess what he was going to ask, and I knew I might lose him if I turned him down too many times.</p>
<p>My mind ticked over possibilities.  What I needed, what I had to do.</p>
<p>Before he could venture a question, I interrupted him.  &#8220;You want in?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;In?&#8221; he asked.  Now he was wary.</p>
<p>I reached beneath my cloak, and I fished out a coinpurse.  Two fingers reached in, and came out fully extended, two dollars in coins pressed between the tips.</p>
<p>The wariness subsided.</p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;ll give you this on good faith.  Eight whole dollars if you follow through.  I need you to do something for me.&#8221;</p>
<p>He reached for and claimed the money without any hesitation.</p>
<p>&#8220;You said you had friends?&#8221; I asked.</p>
<p>&#8220;Sure.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;On top of the grocer&#8217;s place. Corner of Oxbow and Halls.  Wait there.  Take turns keeping an eye out.  You&#8217;re looking for a black coach, led by two stitched horses, heading toward the Academy.  You&#8217;ll know they&#8217;re stitched because they&#8217;re wearing raincoats.  Won&#8217;t be more than two hours&#8217; wait.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Uh huh?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;There&#8217;s a rain barrel up there.  They&#8217;re going to have to stop to wait for the way to clear before they can carry on their way.  What you&#8217;re going to do is tip over the barrel.  Send water off the edge of the roof, onto the horses if you can.  Might want to prop some things up around the barrel, to make sure it happens.&#8221;</p>
<p>He frowned a bit.</p>
<p>&#8220;Ten dollars, all in all, for you and your friends, for one afternoon&#8217;s work.  Pretty good deal.  Don&#8217;t think you can do it?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;I can do it,&#8221; Thom said.</p>
<p>&#8220;You sure?&#8221; I asked.</p>
<p>&#8220;I can do it,&#8221; he said, voice firm.</p>
<p>I studied him, head to toe, taking it all in.</p>
<p>Reaching beneath my cloak, I collected a note from a pocket.  I pressed it into his hands.</p>
<p>He looked down at the money, stunned.</p>
<p>&#8220;If you don&#8217;t follow through, you won&#8217;t get a deal like this again,&#8221; I said.  &#8220;Think <em>hard</em> before you try cheating me.  A big part of what we do is find people.&#8221;</p>
<p>Mute, he nodded.</p>
<p>&#8220;Go,&#8221; I said.</p>
<p>He went, running, feet splashing in puddles of water.</p>
<p>Lillian was about halfway down.</p>
<p>&#8220;You lied to him,&#8221; Jamie said.</p>
<p>&#8220;Would you rather I told the truth?&#8221; I asked.</p>
<p>&#8220;If you&#8217;re going to get him involved.&#8221;</p>
<p>I shook my head.</p>
<p>&#8220;Which leads me to ask&#8230; what are you up to?&#8221; Jamie asked.  &#8220;You weren&#8217;t just getting rid of him or making trouble.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;m going inside,&#8221; I said, starting for the door.  &#8220;Tell the others if they&#8217;re wondering.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;That&#8217;s not what I&#8217;m asking,&#8221; Jamie said.</p>
<p>But he didn&#8217;t move from his spot, and I was already gone.</p>
<p>I passed under Lillian a second time, peeking up her skirt a second time, more to needle her than out of any lingering curiosity.  The big door was, as it turned out, locked, and I wasn&#8217;t able to bypass the big padlock any more than I could bypass the latch of the window above.  But the door rolled on wheels, and the wheels fit in ruts, a long, shallow channel.</p>
<p>I headed to the end of the door opposite the lock, and I pushed the full weight of my body against it.  The lock rattled, heavy.</p>
<p>I tried a bit more pressure, pushing, and the door tilted, the bottom corner closest to me rising out of the channel.  Gripping the door, I lifted it up and away, wood scraping concrete as I created a triangular gap.</p>
<p>I slipped inside, my eyes immediately going to the hayloft, the upper floor.</p>
<p>Helen was there, sitting with one foot propped up, both hands in her lap to keep her skirt pressed down.  Her face was still expressionless.  Half of her attention was on me.  Half was on the creature.  There was a rain barrel beside her, rigged so it hung over the edge of the hayloft, collecting the water that ran in through the makeshift gutter, feeding a steady stream down into containers below.  Runoff from those containers fed into the corner of the building.  A drain from when the building had been a warehouse, keeping the goods dry.</p>
<p>I studied the papers on the desk.  Water from one of the windows above spat down.  Barely large enough to qualify as drops, but they dotted one paper, making ink bleed.  Sketches of the beast.  Notations on structure and anatomy.</p>
<p>One of the texts on a table beside the desk was hand-made.  Pages had holes in them, and a cord was laced through, tying them to the heavy leather cover.  With care, I paged through the thing.</p>
<p>One being, knit together from several.  The better traits of each, all drawn together.  References to Wollstone&#8217;s texts, to the ratios of life, and to the volumes of genetic code for Felidae and <em>Eunectes Murinus</em>.</p>
<p>A whole chapter on digestive enzymes.  Diagrams of the thing&#8217;s fangs, which I had glimpsed as it devoured the mother cat, with labels for the reservoirs of venom that wasn&#8217;t true venom.  It was enzymes, much like the ones bugs used to dissolve their meals before supping them.  Notes suggested that the feature helped with the digestion of any and all food.</p>
<p>Little doubt of what this thing had been engineered for.</p>
<p>My finger traced the labels of glass containers, bottles and vials.  Blood, bile, cerebral fluid&#8230;</p>
<p>Venom.  I&#8217;d expected it to be green, but it was clear, in a glass container with a murky exterior, about as tall and wide as a wine bottle, though more cylindrical.</p>
<p>There was a noise at the door, and I took a long step to the side, toward the shadows beside one of the big wooden containers for water.</p>
<p>Only Lillian, followed by Gordon, passing through the gap.  Gordon was the largest of us, and it was a particularly tight fit for him.</p>
<p>I continued paging through the text.</p>
<p>Diet.</p>
<p>My eye traveled down the list.  Meal times, meal sources, meal sizes.</p>
<p style="padding-left:30px;"><em>Pig carcass.<br />
Dog carcass.<br />
Pig carcass.<br />
Scavenged meal, unknown type.</em><br />
<em>Pig carcass.</em><br />
<em>Pig carcass.</em><br />
<em>Scavenged meal, dog.</em></p>
<p>It wasn&#8217;t fully grown, but it was close, and it grew fast.  Two meals a week.</p>
<p>I recalled that it had eaten the cat, and then looked back at the entries.</p>
<p><em>Forty pounds, sixty pounds, forty pounds, est. one hundred pounds&#8230;  </em>I noted the numbers, and tried to find the pattern between those numbers and the meals.</p>
<p>I moved ahead a few pages until I reached the first partial page.  Room left for more entries.</p>
<p>Last meal, just over two days ago, goat carcass.  It was hungry already.  Quite possibly getting ready for one last growth spurt.  The more recent meals were larger.</p>
<p>Gordon was crouched, peering at labels on bottles.  He saw me looking, and tapped his nose, then pointed at the bottles</p>
<p>I nodded.</p>
<p>I tapped the book, getting his attention, and stepped away while he read the entries.</p>
<p>He didn&#8217;t have much of a chance to read.</p>
<p>There was a sound outside, violent, of things falling over.  Chaos.</p>
<p>I could picture Jamie&#8217;s hiding spot, the way the door had been propped up.  This was a warning.</p>
<p>&#8220;<em>Hide</em>,&#8221; Gordon whispered.</p>
<p><em>You don&#8217;t have to tell me</em>, I thought, but I held my tongue.</p>
<p>Very carefully, I closed the book.  I shifted the angle to return it to the position it had been in.  My eye swept over the room.</p>
<p>Water on the floor.  Did it matter?</p>
<p>No.  There was no time, besides.</p>
<p>I slipped into the shadowy crevice between the water tank and the wall.  Gordon and Lillian were already gone.  Helen, who had been above, watching everything, was now gone.  No doubt hiding behind the water barrel, a step away from where she had been.</p>
<p>Four seconds passed before I heard the lock rattle.</p>
<p>The door&#8217;s wheel slammed back down into the rut as it was pulled to one side, but there was no sign of concern or suspicion.</p>
<p>He closed the door behind him, and the sound of something being dragged joined the sound of hard shoe soles on the wooden floor, marking his progress across his makeshift laboratory.</p>
<p>&#8220;Damned beast,&#8221; he muttered.  &#8220;Where are you?&#8221;</p>
<p>He made seemingly deliberate noise as he cleared a table, then dropped his burden on top of it.</p>
<p>I heard a grunt, his, and the smell of blood filled the air.</p>
<p>The amount of light in the room shifted.  I judged it to be the beast&#8217;s bulk blocking the light from the windows above.</p>
<p>&#8220;There you are,&#8221; he said.</p>
<p>With swift strides, he crossed to the water tank I crouched beside.  He wasted no time in dipping his hands inside, splashing water as he swished his hands inside.  Some of the water that slopped around the top of the tank splashed down on top of me.</p>
<p>I was close enough to touch him.</p>
<p>There was a scuffle and a thud as the cat-snake creature touched ground, eager to get to its meal.  Its creator was already at the desk, picking select vials, dabbing a bit on his wrist, then rubbing his wrists together.</p>
<p>I thought of Gordon&#8217;s gesture.  Touching his nose.</p>
<p><em>Scents?</em></p>
<p><em>Pheromones</em>.</p>
<p>It was how he controlled the beast he had made.</p>
<p>I could see him as he tidied papers, only periodically glancing over his shoulder.  He hummed.  But for some stubble on his chin, he looked like a gentleman, with a four-button vest under a butcher&#8217;s apron and an ankle-length raincoat.  His hair was sandy, parted to one side.</p>
<p>I could see the creature raise its head.  The meal was in its mouth, and it was angling its head to let it all slide down its gullet.</p>
<p>Its creator used a pair of tongs to collect a bloody sack.  I took it to be the sack the creature&#8217;s meal had been in.  Another pig, perhaps.</p>
<p>He disappeared from view.</p>
<p>A rustle.</p>
<p>Then the tongs clanged to the floor.  The beast changed the angle of its head.</p>
<p>&#8220;A <em>child</em>?&#8221; the man&#8217;s voice was touched with incredulity.</p>
<p>There was a commotion, a scrape of steel on concrete as a foot dragged on the tongs.</p>
<p>I remained where I was.</p>
<p>The struggle continued, intermittent, as he backed up, the desk of papers to one side, the table of bottles to the other.  He had a carving knife to Gordon&#8217;s throat.  Presumably the same one he&#8217;d used to cut open the creature&#8217;s meal and get its attention.</p>
<p>&#8220;Two of you.  Are there more?&#8221;</p>
<p>Gordon was silent.</p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;m asking you!&#8221; the man was angry, outraged.  &#8220;Are there <em>more?  </em>Girl!  How many?  Tell me or I cut him!&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;A few,&#8221; Lillian said.  &#8220;Four.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;The noise outside.  That was one?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Five, if you count him,&#8221; she said, her voice small.</p>
<p>&#8220;Do <em>not</em> play games with me!&#8221; the man roared.  &#8220;Show yourselves!  Each of you!&#8221;</p>
<p>I exhaled slowly.</p>
<p>I stepped out of the gap by the water tank.</p>
<p>Helen was above, at the hayloft.  Standing by the edge.  Lillian was closer to the door.  She and Gordon had been hiding in or near a garbage bin.</p>
<p>The beast was relaxed, having just eaten its fill.</p>
<p>&#8220;<em>Children?</em>&#8221; the man sounded incredulous.</p>
<p>He wasn&#8217;t wrong.  At thirteen, Lillian was the oldest of us.  Gordon was only twelve as of last month.</p>
<p>&#8220;Yeah,&#8221; Gordon said, his voice strained.  The man had his throat caught in the crook of one arm, exposing his lower throat.</p>
<p>&#8220;An <em>infestation</em>,&#8221; the man said.  &#8220;My experiment didn&#8217;t root you out?&#8221;</p>
<p>His eye traveled over each of us in turn.  I saw the faintest crease appear between his eyebrows.</p>
<p>He seemed to come to a realization.  &#8220;You&#8217;ve covered yourself in something.  So it can&#8217;t smell you.  This was premeditated.&#8221;</p>
<p>I met Lillian&#8217;s eyes.  I jerked my chin.  Pointed at her with my hand.</p>
<p>The easy, natural interactions and cooperation that followed from years of working together weren&#8217;t there with Lil.  She was new.  A recent addition to the group.</p>
<p>I almost thought she got the wrong idea, until she opened her mouth.</p>
<p>&#8220;Yes,&#8221; she said.  &#8220;We&#8230; heard about you.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Heard what?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;That there was something loose in the slums.  It was eating pets.  It ate a man that was sleeping outside.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;No,&#8221; the man said.</p>
<p>&#8220;Yes,&#8221; Lillian said.  &#8220;There are witnesses.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;The witnesses are wrong,&#8221; the man said.</p>
<p>&#8220;You let it go out to find its own food,&#8221; Gordon said, his voice still strangled.  &#8220;You couldn&#8217;t afford to keep it fed as it grew this large.  You let it feed on strays.  Which it did.  Except one of those strays was human.  It&#8217;s in the book.  Meal, unknown type.&#8221;</p>
<p>I edged around behind the man.</p>
<p>&#8220;You don&#8217;t know what you&#8217;re talking about.  I&#8217;ve studied its leavings,&#8221; the man said, ignoring the label.  &#8220;Nothing human.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Nothing <em>conclusively</em> human, you mean,&#8221; Gordon said.  &#8220;But you aren&#8217;t able to identify all of what it ate.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;You!&#8221;  The man raised his voice.  He sounded more emotional than before.  &#8220;Up there!  Girl!  Stay put.&#8221;</p>
<p>Helen froze where she was.</p>
<p>&#8220;You&#8217;re a killer,&#8221; Gordon said, more insistent.  &#8220;We were calling you the snake charmer.&#8221;</p>
<p>I edged closer to the table.</p>
<p>I didn&#8217;t make a sound, but the snake charmer sensed trouble before it arrived.  He wheeled on me, the knife dangerously close to Gordon&#8217;s throat.</p>
<p>I lunged in the same movement, seizing the big bottle.  The venom.  I held it high.</p>
<p>&#8220;You don&#8217;t care what happens to him?&#8221; the snake charmer asked.</p>
<p>&#8220;I care,&#8221; I said.  &#8220;That&#8217;s why, if you cut him, and if it looks like he isn&#8217;t going to be okay, I&#8217;m going to throw this at the both of you.&#8221;</p>
<p>The snake charmer&#8217;s eyes darted around.  He couldn&#8217;t watch all of us at once.</p>
<p>&#8220;Move!&#8221; he said.  &#8220;Go around.  I want you as a group.&#8221;</p>
<p>I didn&#8217;t budge.</p>
<p>&#8220;Move!&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;<em>No</em>,&#8221; I said.</p>
<p>&#8220;It&#8217;s over, snake charmer,&#8221; Gordon said.</p>
<p>&#8220;That is not my name!&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;It&#8217;s a name they&#8217;ll give you,&#8221; Gordon said.  &#8220;They&#8217;ll make you a monster.  It&#8217;s what the Academy does.  Dehumanizes the dangerous ones.  You can&#8217;t get all of us, not with the way things are, here.  Some are bound to escape.  They&#8217;ll tell people, and those people will find you.  You know the resources the Academy has.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;No,&#8221; the snake charmer said.</p>
<p>&#8220;You don&#8217;t know?&#8221; Gordon asked.</p>
<p>&#8220;This is <em>not</em> my fault,&#8221; the snake charmer said.  &#8220;The Academy&#8230; this rests on their shoulders, not mine.  You can&#8217;t enroll without showing your skill, and you can&#8217;t show your skill without experimenting, but oh, no, they don&#8217;t <em>allow</em> that, do they?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;There are ways,&#8221; Lillian said.</p>
<p>&#8220;No!&#8221; the man barked, &#8220;No!  Not nearly enough.  The world is changing, and <em>they&#8217;re</em> deciding the course.  <em>They&#8217;re</em> putting us in this situation, where risks have to be taken.  Gambles have to be made, or history will continue to be made, names attached to great discoveries, and the rest of us?  If we&#8217;re lucky, we get left by the wayside.  If we&#8217;re not, we&#8217;re just fuel for what <em>they&#8217;re </em>setting in motion.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;m a student there,&#8221; Lillian said.  &#8220;I just started, but&#8230; I&#8217;m enrolled.  First year of study.  Not them.  Just me.&#8221;</p>
<p>I could see the man&#8217;s expression twist.  Incomprehension.  Comprehension, which was almost worse.  Hatred for a thirteen year old girl.</p>
<p>Then rage, not a clean, pure kind, but one that only drove him further into a corner.</p>
<p>His hand tightened on the grip of the knife.</p>
<p>I figured out the destination he was arriving at before he did.</p>
<p>I arrived at my own, and I mustered up some courage.</p>
<p>Very deliberately, I grunted, heaving the bottle of acid at the snake charmer.</p>
<p>He heard the grunt, but so did Gordon.  With the snake charmer&#8217;s attention caught between Lillian and me, Gordon found a chance to protect his throat, keeping the knife from cutting.</p>
<p>The bottle flew lazily through the air.  Gordon ducked, head down, and the snake charmer released him.</p>
<p>The man caught the bottle in a bear hug.</p>
<p>He stared down at the container.</p>
<p>All the same emotions he&#8217;d felt for Lillian, now aimed at me, progressing much faster this time.  Incomprehension, comprehension, hatred, rage.</p>
<p>Directed at me.</p>
<p>I backed away, stumbling, falling.  I covered my face as he swung, using the waterproof cloth to try and shield my body.</p>
<p>He didn&#8217;t throw at me, but at the floor.  The chance of me catching it was small, but by throwing it at the floor, he could guarantee that the bottle would shatter into a spray I couldn&#8217;t possibly shield all of myself from.</p>
<p>The pain was sharp, at first, droplets touching skin, immediately breaking it down.  Then it burned.</p>
<p>The horrible <em>coldness</em> was worse, because it suggested dying nerves.  All down my arms, and one side of my face.</p>
<p>I screamed.</p>
<p>The creature turned its head, but didn&#8217;t move.</p>
<p>The man turned, wheeling on the others.  Gordon was ready, already closing in, taking advantage of the short moment it took the snake charmer to adjust his grip on the knife, so soon after heaving the bottle.</p>
<p>A tackle, shoulder into the man&#8217;s gut, taking advantage of smaller size and a good physique.  Gordon drove the man back.</p>
<p>Gordon was the hero, golden haired, noble, likeable.  Talented.</p>
<p>When he broke away, letting the snake charmer stumble back two steps, recovering balance, Gordon had the knife in hand.</p>
<p>The beast rose to its feet.  Sniffing.</p>
<p>I managed to stop screaming, going as still as possible.</p>
<p>It still edged closer to me.  Interested.</p>
<p><em>Still hungry,  </em>I noted.</p>
<p>Helen acted.  Tipping over the barrel.</p>
<p>Drenching the snake charmer, washing away his charm, the pheromones.</p>
<p>&#8220;Brats!&#8221; the snake charmer spat the word.  &#8220;You little <em>shits</em>!  You think you have control of this situation?&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Your experiment is trying to decide between you and Sylvester over there,&#8221; Gordon said.  &#8220;You smell, he&#8217;s bleeding.  Both are tempting.&#8221;</p>
<p>The snake charmer made an incoherent noise.</p>
<p>&#8220;Thing is, if you start bleeding&#8230;&#8221; Gordon said, trailing off.  &#8220;You&#8217;ll suddenly be a lot more tempting.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Try it,&#8221; the snake charmer said.</p>
<p>Gordon did.  He approached, and the snake charmer tried to grab him.</p>
<p>The man&#8217;s hands only grabbed clothes.  A hood and cloak meant to keep the rain off.  Gordon let him, and ducked low, the clothing bunching up around his neck and upper chest.</p>
<p>Gordon sliced the snake charmer&#8217;s stomach.  A shallow cut.</p>
<p>Another grab, wrestling Gordon, trying to overpower with strength, seizing one arm.</p>
<p>Gordon let the knife drop out of one hand, falling into the palm of another.</p>
<p>He cut the back of the man&#8217;s left knee.  When the man fell, screaming, Gordon cut the other knee.  He skipped back as the snake charmer fell.</p>
<p>The snake stirred, its attention no longer predominantly on me.</p>
<p>I could see the snake charmer realizing the same thing I had minutes ago.  He knew his experiment.  He knew how it hunted.  It scavenged, sniffing out prey.  Blind, it reacted to noise and smell.  Minimizing the noise one made was vital.</p>
<p>Given the situation, however, staying silent spelled the man&#8217;s doom.  Already, his creation was sniffing him out.  He smelled of blood.</p>
<p>&#8220;Pheromones,&#8221; he said, knowing how dangerous it was to speak, that every sound helped him lose the tug of war that let the creature decide between devouring him and devouring <em>me</em>.  &#8220;Let me- I&#8217;ll come with you.  You can take me in.  You win.&#8221;</p>
<p>Nobody moved or responded.</p>
<p>He used his arms to pull himself forward, progressing toward the table.  Each motion drew more attention from his beast.</p>
<p>Foot by foot, he closed on the table, and each sound was akin to a fisherman&#8217;s line, reeling in the beast.</p>
<p>He reached the table, struggling, and he raised himself up, using one hand to drag a leg forward, propping it under him.  Reaching across the table-</p>
<p>Gordon kicked the leg of the table, hard.  The table shifted a foot, and the snake charmer collapsed.</p>
<p>&#8220;No.  Please.&#8221;</p>
<p>The snake charmer looked at us.  At Gordon, then Helen, who loomed above, perched on the hayloft.  At me, as I glared at him, my face burned.  At Lillian, who was sitting in the corner, hands over her head.</p>
<p>Who was not one of us.</p>
<p>&#8220;Please,&#8221; he said.  &#8220;Not like this.&#8221;</p>
<p>Helen&#8217;s expression didn&#8217;t change.  Gordon shifted his position, placing himself between the snake charmer and the table, arms folded.  I remained where I was, limp and breathing hard.</p>
<p>I could see it dawn on the man.  Comprehension settling in as he realized what he was dealing with.</p>
<p>The snake seized the man&#8217;s feet, and began the very slow process of swallowing him.</p>
<p>The snake charmer&#8217;s screams became frantic.</p>
<p>&#8220;Lillian,&#8221; Gordon said, raising his voice to be heard over the screams.</p>
<p>&#8220;I don&#8217;t want to see.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Then shield your eyes.  But your job is to keep us in one piece.  Sly is hurt.  Focus, and make sure he doesn&#8217;t die.&#8221;</p>
<p>I felt the burning stop as Lillian tended to me.  By the time she was done, the screams had stopped.  The powder that dusted me made it hard to see, but that was fine.  I was lifted to my feet.</p>
<p>&#8220;I have to say, I&#8217;m very interested in what the fuck you were doing, faking that fall, setting yourself up to get hurt just now,&#8221; Gordon said.  &#8220;You&#8217;ll have to tell me later, when you can talk again.&#8221;</p>
<p>I managed a nod.</p>
<p>&#8220;Off we go,&#8221; he said.</p>
<p>I could hear the door open.</p>
<p>Helen spoke for the first time in a while.  Her voice was cute.  &#8220;The Academy sends its regards, Mr. Snake Charmer.&#8221;</p>
<p><span style="color:#ffffff;">.</span>                                                                                                                                                      <strong><a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2015/03/14/taking-root-1-2/">Next</a></strong></p>
			</div><!-- .entry-content -->

			<div class="comment-content"><p>WAG-time &#8211; the updated title images will correspond with the theme of growth moving from taking root all the way through to full growth and blooming (for good or for ill).</p>
<p>Strange fruit comes to mind with the current image.  Also &#8211; the dangers of tasting the fruit of the tree of knowledge.  Unravelling the secrets of life seem like the forbidden fruit has already been eaten and the garden is contaminated.</p>
<p>Open question &#8211; will the growth theme include the inevitable decay and destruction of all life?  Will it follow through to rebirth?</p>
</div>

					<img alt='' src='https://0.gravatar.com/avatar/fdd7a6e5be1afc1211d8fc9ce6d724b1?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn"><a href='http://gravatar.com/prometheus163' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>prometheus163</a></cite> |
					<span class="comment-meta commentmetadata">

			<div class="comment-content"><p>So now that I&#8217;ve read it, thoughts-</p>
<p>I&#8217;m getting the feeling that Wildbow is already working to fix some of the larger complaints many people, myself included, had about Pact. Namely, we have strong protagonistic secondary characters right off the bat instead of halfway through the serial which is good, and we have a character who is familiar with the world around him and how it functions, instead of the readers having to watch a steep learning curve that never really stops. These are huge points to Twig in my book already.</p>
<p>Other thoughts- The remarkably young age of this group is making me a little apprehensive, I&#8217;m definitely one who leans toward older characters. It may be challenging to write about characters so young, especially in what appears to be a very dark setting, and make the audience relate to them well. That being said, I have a huge amount of confidence in Wildbow. If anyone can pull this off, he can!</p>
<p>Also, biopunk, boil, blah blah blah</p>
</div>

</div>

				<div class="comment-author vcard">

			<div class="comment-content"><p>&#8220;Biopunk&#8221; isn&#8217;t really a thing. It&#8217;s like Cyberpunk in the same way that Steampunk is like Cyberpunk which is to say not very much alike. More of a blood and guts style with body horror setting than anything else.</p>
<p>If I&#8217;m guessing correct that is. Wildbow did mention that he didn&#8217;t want people making genre classification too closely or quickly and that it was best to come into the story with an open mind.</p>
<p>I was mostly referring how it was a return to the setting of Boil which was one of the potential stories that would have followed Worm instead of Pact.</p>
<p>Which makes me happy. Maybe we&#8217;ll one day see a return to the story with the animal masks.</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>I&#8217;m pretty sure Sly <i>isn&#8217;t</i> 10 or 11. What&#8217;s the point of biopunk physics if you can&#8217;t reverse your biological age (or at least slow down the aging process)?<br />
Especially if the academy is accepting people of the snake charmer&#8217;s age</p>
<p>Also re: &#8216;what is biopunk&#8217; &#8211; any sci-fi setting that functions off of alchemy-like or genetic engineering (or some sort of virus or evolution&#8230;)-based advances, rather than mechanical advances.<br />
For example, instead of cars we got recombinant horses that eat coal and breathe fire<br />
*can also have mechanical advances if their primary use is changing the function of a biological creature i.e. now you have a steam-powered horse</p>
<p>Very happy btw, liked Boil very much (except for the story line that was being set up)</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>My reading:  Of the &#8216;kids&#8217;, only Lillian is a normal person.  The others are constructs, like the monster (only obviously much more capable of passing.)  Hence why Lillian was there &#8212; she&#8217;s a student, and her job is to oversee the constructs the Academy uses to hunt down rogues, repair them if they get damaged while working, and so on.  That&#8217;s why he emphasizes that Lillian wasn&#8217;t &#8220;one of them.&#8221;</p>
</div>

					<img alt='' src='https://2.gravatar.com/avatar/8bd30c533e1650ac415eb30860c3c7f4?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn">Ganurath</cite> |

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Labels can be dangerous. They can cause you to make assumptions which lead to unexpected surprises. It&#8217;s better to simply observe, take notes, figure out what you&#8217;re dealing with, and try not to get disemboweled.</p>
</div>

			<div class="reply">
				<a rel='nofollow' class='comment-reply-link' href='https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/?replytocom=81#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-81", "81", "respond", "14" )' aria-label='Reply to Ganurath'>Reply</a>			</div><!-- .reply -->
		</article><!-- #comment-## -->

	<li class="comment byuser comment-author-abillyhiggins even thread-even depth-1 highlander-comment" id="li-comment-46">
		<article id="comment-46" class="comment">
			<footer>
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
					<img alt='' src='https://0.gravatar.com/avatar/00233592f5e489ac6f76885f925c6da5?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn"><a href='https://kindasupergay.wordpress.com/' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>abillyhiggins</a></cite> |
					<span class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
						<a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/#comment-46"><time pubdate datetime="2015-03-10T00:51:55+00:00">
						March 10, 2015 at 12:51 am						</time></a>
											</span><!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->

				</div><!-- .comment-author .vcard -->
							</footer>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Interesting stuff. Didn&#8217;t realize it was in the same world as the Boil sample until about halfway down. I&#8217;ve got a couple random thoughts:</p>
<p>&#8211; Anyone find the combo of a vest and butcher&#8217;s apron kind of funny? Made the whole thing seem a lil darker, since you&#8217;ve got this foppish man wearing a butcher&#8217;s apron &#8212; the apron being something that&#8217;s more associated with the working class, which contrasts with the upper-class vest. And of course, the upper class basically &#8216;butchering&#8217; the lower class is a common idea in a lot of British Lit.</p>
<p>&#8211; Which brings me to my next point: I know Wildbow is Canadian, but for some reason this post felt British to me. Maybe it&#8217;s just where my head is at, but the combination of wild-but-precocious schoolchildren, class issues (I might be making that angle up, but it just feels like there&#8217;s something there), and the gentleman-butcher felt British.</p>
<p>&#8211; It&#8217;s way to soon to be talking like this but oh what the hell: Sylvester is already my favorite Wildbow protagonist. I liked Blake and Taylor, but Sylvester&#8217;s a little s*** and I can relate to that.</p>
<p>&#8211; Each of Wildbow&#8217;s serials have started with an italicized thought. This one strikes me as pretty killer. Especially after Pact&#8217;s &#8220;Damn me, damn them, damn it all,&#8221; which left me cold. But this one? Academic and just a lil unsettling.</p>
<p>&#8211; Sylvester&#8217;s hair refuses to be tamed by wax, while Jamie&#8217;s is just long. I&#8217;m curious to unravel the paradox of these wild, feral-seeming children somehow having a connection to The Academy.</p>
<p>&#8211; This isn&#8217;t really much of a thought, but damn I love that Sylvester&#8217;s name is shortened to &#8220;Sly.&#8221; Just&#8230; damn.</p>
<p>&#8211; I&#8217;m glad we&#8217;re starting with an ensemble. Wildbow&#8217;s been pretty good at writing those since Worm, and his dialogue has improved so drastically (I&#8217;d argue it&#8217;s where he&#8217;s shown the most growth).</p>
<p>&#8211; &#8220;The gears were shifting in his head.&#8221; That line really says a lot about Sylvester&#8217;s industrialized way of thinking. Can&#8217;t wait to see more of this world.</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Possible Typos:</p>
<p>I let the droplets where they would<br />
I let the droplets fall where they would</p>
<p>It was twice the size of a large horse, it had crawled up from the space below to the overhanging floor, winding its way up the pillar as a snake might.<br />
more normal to separate the first clause and the rest with a semicolon (or period):<br />
It was twice the size of a large horse; it had crawled up from the space below to the overhanging floor, winding its way up the pillar as a snake might.</p>
<p>I said  “But who told<br />
two spaces after &#8216;said&#8217; &#8211; either should be one or a period</p>
<p>Reactions:</p>
<p>So we are back to what I thought of as the Frankenstein world Boil, where people can put together mad abominations of flesh. That wasn&#8217;t my favorite, but it wasn&#8217;t my last choice either.</p>
<p>It is really hardcore to have four children stand around while a man gets eaten by a cat-snake, even if it did eat a person. These kids are seriously screwed up in the head for them to do this.</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Damnit, I arrived late. Probably gonna be doing that a lot. Ah well, time for some MOSTLY BASELESS THEORIES!</p>
<p>Theory 1: Lilian is Gordon&#8217;s sister. Just that scene where he glares at Sly after the skirt thing. Could be a whole other thing, like Gordon just not liking little shits. Also, I feel the need to point out that I&#8217;ve already almost written &#8220;Grue&#8221; twice, and I&#8217;m imagining him as such, and there&#8217;s nothing you can do about it.<br />
Theory 2: Helen is full up with some of that Academy whizshit, and is as such an insanely powerful biobot. Though I sincerely hope that if this is the case, she won&#8217;t be stereotypical about it. But Wildbow doesn&#8217;t let us down, so there&#8217;s thar to look forward to.<br />
Really, there&#8217;s lots to look forward to, especially if my Theory 3—There Will Be Gambit Pileups—ends up being true. With more defined rules than Pact and gooefully fewer gamebreakers than Worm (not that those weren&#8217;t great, just not feeling a Ziz attack on the Academy, you know?), and the fact that Wildbow is excellent at the whole creativity thing, well&#8230;it practically begs to happen. Multiple times. Wildbow pls.</p>
<p>Oh, and if there&#8217;s no Riley reference, I will cry. But we know there will be references, and I&#8217;m just anticipating the comment/Reddit post where somebody realizes that some Worm/Pact quote was referencing Twig.</p>
<p>Okay, I need to sleep. Awesome first chapter, Wildbow. Saturday can&#8217;t come soon enough. Thank you, and have a good night.</p>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Hooooooooooooooly shit this started dark. It caught me by surprise. I was not once bothered by the darkness of Pact as many others were, and I even found some of the chapters of Worm darker due to bullying. But Twig started dark right of the bat. Little concerned things will get worse too quickly, but it&#8217;s the first chapter, so I&#8217;ll reserve my judgments for later. Same thing with the protagonist; snarky &#8220;little shits&#8221; don&#8217;t sit well with me, but I&#8217;m sure that as he develops I&#8217;ll like him more. Love the banner art, by the way. It&#8217;s quite nightmarish, terrifying and overall amazing.</p>
<p>Aside from the shock, I found the first half a bit hard to read. I didn&#8217;t feel as though I were trying to figure out a mystery as with the start of Pact, but more as though I was dredging through tiresome words for the smallest piece of context. Once context arrived, though, everything was fine and dandy!</p>
<p>The story really has me horrified, in a good way. If that was your goal, congratulations. I am bloody terrified of what will come next. Can&#8217;t wait for the next update!</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>The kitties made me sad. Wildbow starts his stories killing kittens! What the fuck.</p>
<p>And yeah, the skirt thing got me thinking that too. Why was Lillian so worried that Sly would get a look up her skirt? I assumed she was wearing tights or something underneath. Are they temperature-resistant? I think the girls wearing skirts instead of pants refers to the time period (it wasn&#8217;t socially acceptable for girls to wear pants) and culture, generally, rather than a preference (as you said, it doesn&#8217;t make sense).</p>
<p>As for the protagonist, I can&#8217;t say I&#8217;m surprised&#8230; was already expecting someone who makes me groan internally more often than not (such as was the case with Blake and me. Rereading Worm, Taylor&#8217;s narration was like a breath of fresh air), but it&#8217;s clear to me that Sylvester isn&#8217;t Blake 2.0 (or Taylor 2.0, for that matter). That already makes him very interesting (duh!!). </p>
<p>Yay (not in a label-y way) biopunk!</p>

</div>

			<div class="reply">
				<a rel='nofollow' class='comment-reply-link' href='https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/?replytocom=147#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-147", "147", "respond", "14" )' aria-label='Reply to Glassware'>Reply</a>			</div><!-- .reply -->

	<li class="comment even thread-even depth-1 highlander-comment" id="li-comment-94">
		<article id="comment-94" class="comment">
			<footer>
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
					<img alt='' src='https://1.gravatar.com/avatar/439a1776b97b30635441ec6a5dabc14b?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn">Aname</cite> |
					<span class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
						<a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/#comment-94"><time pubdate datetime="2015-03-10T03:15:27+00:00">
						March 10, 2015 at 3:15 am						</time></a>
											</span><!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->

				</div><!-- .comment-author .vcard -->
							</footer>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>There was a brief point in history, sometime victorian iirc, when &#8220;scientists&#8221; were trying to reanimate dead bodies with varying degrees of &#8220;success&#8221;. The most notable (and dubious) such case I know of was two dogs that were supposedly reanimated, although other dogs were seriously freaked out by them and wouldn&#8217;t go anywhere near them. They apparently perished after two weeks or so. At around the same time, other weird things were being done like someone grafting a dog&#8217;s head onto another dog to create a two-headed dog. It didn&#8217;t last long, either. I think at least one of those happened in Germany, but I can&#8217;t for the life of me recall any important names involved.</p>
<p>Of course, if those crazy experiments had been successful instead of failures (or banned on account of holy what, as the case may be), to call the results revolutionary would be an extreme understatement. At the very least, it&#8217;d obviate the entire prosthetics branch of medical practice and half the necessary developments for surgical practice besides. Lost your arm in a farming accident? Just graft a new arm on. Near-sighted? Shell out some cash for normal-sighted eyes. Longer legs for the aspiring runner. The extremely crazy or aspiring crazy might even consider adding more brains to their brains. And then there&#8217;s the fully customized make-your-own-monsters potential, or very specialized work beasts that could theoretically outperform industrial machines. It would be an interesting angle to write a story from, I imagine.</p>
<p>At least, that&#8217;s the first thing that went through my mind when reading the opening chapter of Twig. The second thing was &#8220;we already have a number of characters with pre-established relationships to explore alongside the world and story itself unfolding&#8221; and the third thing was &#8220;whoops, those kids just murdered someone by proxy by letting a giant cat-snake swallow him alive. First chapter and they already have a body count.&#8221;</p>
<p>Wildbow, please forgive me if I pegged this all wrong. It&#8217;s awfully early in the morning here.</p>
</div>

			<div class="reply">
				<a rel='nofollow' class='comment-reply-link' href='https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/?replytocom=94#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-94", "94", "respond", "14" )' aria-label='Reply to Aname'>Reply</a>			</div><!-- .reply -->
		</article><!-- #comment-## -->

			<footer>
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
					<img alt='' src='https://2.gravatar.com/avatar/861594425443be50574697d6f122afd8?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn">Anonymous</cite> |
					<span class="comment-meta commentmetadata">

			<div class="comment-content"><p>YESSSS!</p>
<p>Really happy to see the world of Boil again for Twig.  I wondered at the start, then wondered if it was a post-apocalyptic story instead, then wondered some more&#8230;  very happy.</p>
<p>I also liked the age-bracket reveal.</p>
<p>Let&#8217;s see, notes/suggestions:</p>
<p>Sulfur aside, pretty sure we can&#8217;t have life as we know it without phosphorus (ATP as energy currency, and the phosphate-(deoxy)ribose backbone in DNA/RNA).  Granted, if the protagonist/viewpoint is intended to be imitating scientific knowledge without knowing much of it then leaving out two of the six elements.  Then again, it&#8217;s referred to as &#8216;the first lesson&#8217; (as though an official piece of knowledge filtered down to others), so odd for it be missing a third.</p>
<p>Similarly, the application of that first lesson to the situation at hand seemed wonky, though that makes more sense given the protagonist&#8217;s/viewpoint&#8217;s perspective.  Even if carbon is implied by the solid objects, and hydrogen is implied by the water, &#8216;The air around us was stale, but it was still oxygen&#8217; (rather than &#8216;still contained oxygen&#8217;) ignores its nitrogen content and implies a deep misunderstanding of air&#8217;s composition on the part of the protagonist/viewpoint.</p>
<p>Wallace&#8217;s law that &#8216;every species into comes into existence with a closely related (&#8216;allied&#8217;) species&#8211;not clear how that&#8217;s related to the cat and its litter, though this makes sense if it&#8217;s an unrelated Wallace.  (If so, my prompted curiosity wants to find out what Wallace&#8217;s law is in this context, and/or find and reread Boil to check for other hints.)</p>
<p>In conclusion, thank you very much!  Very happy.  Hoping for lots of interestingness&#8230;  *smiles*</p>
</div>

	<li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 highlander-comment" id="li-comment-124">
		<article id="comment-124" class="comment">
			<footer>
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
					<img alt='' src='https://0.gravatar.com/avatar/ffe8ce12da12cfdb7af753539a263bb1?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn"><a href='http://gravatar.com/alterego9' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Alterego 9</a></cite> |
					<span class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
						<a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/#comment-124"><time pubdate datetime="2015-03-10T09:02:00+00:00">
						March 10, 2015 at 9:02 am						</time></a>
											</span><!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->

				</div><!-- .comment-author .vcard -->
							</footer>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Definitely more more promising than Pact, so far. </p>
<p>The reason why I supported Boil during the test runs, was that it was a lot more Worm-like in that it had a determined, younger protagonist making her own choice to carve out a plot, while Face, Peer, and Pact all had detached twenty-something dudes getting pushed into dangerous situations against their will. </p>
<p>The age and the the gender, were also important factors (there is a reason why people liked Evan, Maggie, and Green Eyes even over Blake. Feminity and/or youth has an appeal that &#8220;some dude&#8221; doesn&#8217;t), but the active participation is the element that is truly necessary for a protagonist.  </p>
<p>So far, it looks like Twig mercifully inherited that aspect of Boil. We don&#8217;t know much about the protagonists, but they are not just victims reacting, they do their own thing. As creepy as the story might get, it hopefully won&#8217;t be just a horror, in the sense of being about constantly running away from monsters.</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Another thing not mentioned so far is the use of the term &#8220;uninitiated&#8221; in the italicized text.  This has echoes of practitioner or cape.  Obgu bs Jvyqobj&#8217;f fgbevrf unir unq fbzrguvat yvxr na ryvgr pynff (r.t. pncrf be cenpgvgvbaref) jvgu gura n ynlre bs gehgu be frpergf haqrecvaavat gung ryvgr.  I suspect the same holds true with the &#8220;initiated&#8221; e.g. likely members of the Academy.  It also has a tone of indicating that even if the kids are not all initiated, they are at least wise to the situation in a deeper way.  They might be in that middle ground of &#8220;witch hunters&#8221; or &#8220;PRT&#8221; members rather than just being muggles, even if all of the kids are not Initiated/Capes/Practitioners.</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>The &#8220;about&#8221; description also mentions a &#8220;great mind&#8221; not a &#8220;great person&#8221; or &#8220;great man/woman&#8221; (The year is 1921, and a little over a century has passed since a great mind unraveled the underpinnings of life itself).  This distinction smells important &#8211; the identity and nature of the original great mind will likely be a key plot point &#8211; how has this mind itself evolved?  From reading HPMOR and Eliezer&#8217;s work, the use of the term &#8220;mind&#8221; brings up thoughts of AI &#8211; friendly or otherwise.  Bio-modifications make it seem likely the Mind has been able to self-improve &#8211; the supposed &#8220;FOOM&#8221; scenario.</p>
<p>Also &#8211; unraveled again feels important.  It has that tone of of component pieces coming apart.  Similar to how the text starts with the required components of life.  It feels a bit like a tinker combining normal elements into something amazing.  With the red imagery of the graphic, I can&#8217;t help but think about Obarfnj&#8217;f erq zvnfzn qvffbyivat rirelguvat arne gur raq bs gur Fynhtugreubhfr Avar nep va Jbez.</p>
<p>Right now &#8211; my head canon says that this is Havirefr Mnlva be fbzr bgure sne syhat Jbez havirefr naq guvf vf nyy gur znq perngvba bs n Obarfnj be Avyobt funeq whzcrq vagb guvf jbeyq fbzr fvtavsvpnag gvzr rneyvre naq npgvat nf gur bayl funeq nyvir va gur fcnpr.</p>
</div>

	<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 highlander-comment" id="li-comment-221">
		<article id="comment-221" class="comment">
			<footer>
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
					<img alt='' src='https://2.gravatar.com/avatar/89781a1fd6993789d338f695c49358dd?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn">Unmaker</cite> |
					<span class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
						<a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/#comment-221"><time pubdate datetime="2015-03-10T17:19:35+00:00">
						March 10, 2015 at 5:19 pm						</time></a>
											</span><!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->

				</div><!-- .comment-author .vcard -->
							</footer>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>I think this plot has been done with a Frankenstein takeoff, but the way I imagine it is: if cobbling together biological beings is so easy, sooner or later someone will cobble together a superior brain. That brain, realizing that knowledge of this sort of thing is <em>absolutely</em> critical to its day-to-day existence, will study the biological sciences intensely. And it might do what just about every other group has done after they have come to power using a new option: close the door after itself, i.e. ban creations similar to itself. And since you can&#8217;t do that by yourself, you have to organize. This would end up with institutions run by highly intelligent abominations who wanted to learn about the biological sciences but at the same time put hard limits and controls on those who use them. Sound familiar?</p>
</div>

			<div class="reply">
				<a rel='nofollow' class='comment-reply-link' href='https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/?replytocom=221#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-221", "221", "respond", "14" )' aria-label='Reply to Unmaker'>Reply</a>			</div><!-- .reply -->

			<footer>
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
					<img alt='' src='https://0.gravatar.com/avatar/30c18c4fe0b8425ff0d3055fb9909b9b?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn"><a href='http://bakerjake.wordpress.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>bakerjake</a></cite> |

						<a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/#comment-322"><time pubdate datetime="2015-03-12T16:32:26+00:00">
						March 12, 2015 at 4:32 pm						</time></a>
											</span><!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->

				</div><!-- .comment-author .vcard -->
							</footer>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Agreed.  Biological improvement by necessity may almost have to deal with recursive self-improvement via superior brain modification, and the resulting disparity vis a vis lesser intelligences.  I don&#8217;t dare try to predict Wildbow, as he always manages to confound expectations, but it seems like there are a lot of interesting narrative reference posts already being laid.  What excites me most is how well Wildbow was able to deconstruct and then rebuild and evaluate something as played over as the superhero genre.  In a world that seems even MORE free form and potentially modifiable such as this one, the scope for changes and deconstruction is very exciting.</p>
</div>

	<li class="comment byuser comment-author-bakerjake odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 highlander-comment" id="li-comment-153">
		<article id="comment-153" class="comment">
			<footer>
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
					<img alt='' src='https://0.gravatar.com/avatar/30c18c4fe0b8425ff0d3055fb9909b9b?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn"><a href='http://bakerjake.wordpress.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>bakerjake</a></cite> |
					<span class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
						<a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/#comment-153"><time pubdate datetime="2015-03-10T13:54:26+00:00">
						March 10, 2015 at 1:54 pm						</time></a>
											</span><!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->

				</div><!-- .comment-author .vcard -->
							</footer>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>As mentioned, water plays a huge role throughout &#8211; I missed this on the first read, but I just saw that the water from the gutters is redirected into the building:</p>
<p>&#8220;Water ran around and below us, flowing over our bare feet, redirected from gutters to the building’s inside.&#8221;</p>
<p>Also &#8211; there is a description of the &#8220;trough&#8221; saying &#8220;with little care given for the leaks here and there,&#8221; &#8211; again conservation and use of water is quite important.</p>
<p>Also &#8211; the wax is described as both smell and water proofing.  Note that Sy does not cover his waxed hair (risk taking?  Not as fully modified as the others?) &#8220;everyone but me flipping up their hoods to ward off the rain.  I let the droplets fall where they would, on hair that refused to be bound down beneath a thick layer of waterproofing wax&#8221;</p>
<p>If Helen is the one on the far right in the photo (covered by an umbrella) that matches with her decision to stay in the barn and avoid the rain, which &#8220;made a degree of sense&#8221;?</p>
<p>[“I stay,” Helen murmured. There was no questioning it, no argument.  We couldn’t afford to make the noise, and it made a degree of sense.]</p>
<p>One thing we really know about Wildbow is that he plots out a lot of very minor details that are all quite relevant in retrospect.  </p>
<p>Another component is that the &#8220;first lesson&#8221; covers life &#8220;flourishing.&#8221;  Again, another quite important word.</p>
<p>When writing about the kittens being eaten, Sy references Wallace&#8217;s law &#8211; possibly referring to this guy: <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace" rel="nofollow">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace</a></p>
<p>That reinforces at least some British connections plus the rough time frame.  </p>
<p>The reference in description of buildings to being planted is interesting: &#8220;buildings scattered like they’d been blown around by strong winds and planted where they lay&#8221;</p>
<p>Also &#8211; &#8220;You’d think the rain would wash away the smell&#8221; in reference to the smell on the Academy and the buildings again reinforces water as cleaning and purifying but the current situation being too much even for that much rain.</p>
<p>Sy being barefoot is also noteworthy &#8211; they are clearly well prepared (backpack full of tools) but Sy didn&#8217;t wear shoes out in the rain while climbing and seeking out monsters?</p>
</div>

<p>&#8220;The way things looked, we were very close to doing the opposite of ‘flourishing’.<br />

	<li class="comment byuser comment-author-bakerjake even thread-even depth-1 highlander-comment" id="li-comment-156">
		<article id="comment-156" class="comment">
			<footer>
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
					<img alt='' src='https://0.gravatar.com/avatar/30c18c4fe0b8425ff0d3055fb9909b9b?s=50&#038;d=identicon&#038;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50' height='50' width='50' />					<cite class="fn"><a href='http://bakerjake.wordpress.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>bakerjake</a></cite> |
					<span class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
						<a href="https://twigserial.wordpress.com/2014/12/24/taking-root-1-1/#comment-156"><time pubdate datetime="2015-03-10T14:01:12+00:00">
						March 10, 2015 at 2:01 pm						</time></a>
											</span><!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->

				</div><!-- .comment-author .vcard -->
							</footer>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>The various &#8220;stitcheds&#8221; (new word for the plural: &#8220;stitches&#8221;) have names when they become infamous:</p>
<p>“Did you hear about the crying man of Butcher’s Row?”<br />
“Sly,” Jamie said, suddenly paying attention to the issue.  The name was a warning.<br />
But Thom gave an answer, “That stitched that went crazy.  Remembered things.”<br />
“That’s the one.  Do you remember Mother Hen?”<br />
Thom nodded.  “That nurse who- the babies.”<br />
He looked rather uneasy now.<br />
“That’s right,” I said, doing my best to sound calm, reassuring.  “The nurse.  Yes.  Both got caught, right?  Everything got tied up neatly?”<br />
“Yeah,” Thom said.  He couldn’t meet my eyes, so he focused on Lillian instead.  “The authorities from the Academy got them.”</p>
<p>Also, at least some of the stitches seem to be made of previous individuals with memories.  </p>
<p>If the authorities need help catching and finding rogue stitches, then things are very much out of hand.  That&#8217;s clear from the snake charmer being caught by the children.  Another interesting fact is that the kids have the autonomy to decide the Academy won&#8217;t care if this guy gets eaten. That also implies the academy might want the snake thing alive (creator probably could better help eliminate snake thing and then be brought in?).  </p>
<p>In Wildbowverses, it seems the &#8220;powers that be&#8221; are often seemingly not fully in control of the situation, and have situations like &#8220;needing kids to do intel and deal with monsters&#8221; for them, when in actuality, that underlying situation is actually an intentional test/experiment/plan.  Gura gur gehr cbjref gung or guvax gurl ner ernyyl chyyvat fgevatf ohg gurl nera&#8217;g npghnyyl va pbageby naq fghss fcvenyf jvyqyl vagb punbf.  Guvax Pnhyqeba be gur zrgn yriry tnzr cynlvat bs Tenaqzn Ebfr naq gur Ynjlref.  Gur Npnqrzl frrzf gb cbgragvnyyl or pbzcnenoyr gb gur Gevhzivengr, jvgu znlor gur &#8216;frperg beqre&#8217; ehaavat gur Npnqrzl yvxr n Pnhyqeba.  </p>
<p>My current bet is that the evolution of the &#8220;mind&#8221; which unravelled the secrets of life is actually behind the first layer of stuff going on and then there is something bigger behind it.</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>I think best practice for Wildbow comments is to avoid too strongly spoiling his other stories.  Easiest way to do this is using Rot13 (<a href="http://www.rot13.com/" rel="nofollow">http://www.rot13.com/</a>) to discuss items from other stories.  For example, the discussion of Gur zhygvirefr va Jbez, gur pbebanf, gur cnffratref, gur qrsvavgvba bs jung gvaxref ner, gur pbafhzcgvba bs bgure jbeyqf, rgp. frrz yvxr ernyyl ovt fcbvyref sbe Jbez.  Gung fnvq, V&#8217;z tynq lbh funerq gur nccerpvngvba sbe gur grez Zvaq naq gur pbaarpgvba gb fbzrguvat Gvaxre yvxr.  </p>
<p>I wont directly @ Wildbow for input, but it might be good for us to collectively agree to &#8220;community guidelines&#8221; on things like spoiler policy.  Certainly no one has bad intents in this, and I think it&#8217;s near 100% certain that people commenting on the FIRST day of the new serial have all read the previous stories.  Just a thought to help keep things unspoilt for those who haven&#8217;t enjoyed the other serials yet.</p>
</div>

							</footer>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Interesting that Lillian &#8220;Who was not one of us&#8221; has the specific job of keeping the rest of the team in one piece.  She seems to be almost to be playing the Panacea/Healer role.  </p>
<p>The lack of empathy on the team&#8217;s part also seems to signal something to the snake charmer: &#8220;I could see it dawn on the man.  The comprehension.  Just what he was dealing with.&#8221;</p>
<p>Maybe there are &#8220;initiated and uninitiated&#8221;, academy members, stitches, experimenters, and then some middle ground patchwork category that the children are part of.  </p>
<p>Injury on Sy&#8217;s part seems to indicate that he can get repairs or upgrades &#8211; it&#8217;s sufficient enough for Gordon to call it out: </p>
<p>“I’m very interested in what the fuck you were doing, faking that fall, setting yourself up to get hurt,” Gordon said.  “You’ll have to tell me later, when you can talk again.”</p>
<p>Sy&#8217;s disregard for water also seems to play into this.  He has a hint of an addict &#8211; a risk taker intentionally trying to get &#8220;more of the good stuff.&#8221;</p>
<p>It&#8217;s also worth noting that Sy as the narrator is actively concealing stuff from us.  Faking the fall is not implied very clearly in the narrated thoughts.  Similarly the waterproofing wax starts out as just being described as a substance.</p>
</div>

			<div class="comment-content"><p>Fair point.  I think the wording strongly indicates that it&#8217;s the one you&#8217;re pointing out.  And even so, as Sy is clearly quite nimble, it makes a fall appear doubly noteworthy.  Assuming it&#8217;s the one you point out, it sort of further emphasizes Sy wanting some form of healing/fixing/upgrades.  </p>
<p>I&#8217;m probably reading too much into a lot of this, but I noticed that both Helen and Gordon are described as golden haired.  Any other golden tinged Wildbow characters of note we might recall?  Curious to have two already described in the same group &#8211; think if BOTH Bitch and Brian had had a huge affection for dogs or something.  Particularly interesting too when the use of color is so muted and drab &#8211; calling out golden for both characters feels notable.</p>
<p>There&#8217;s also a degree of self-loathing embedded in Sy calling out Gordon as &#8220;golden haired&#8221; and the &#8220;hero&#8221; and &#8220;talented&#8221; in the midst of the violent struggle going on.  Sy has just been covered in enzymes and had his face melt, and he&#8217;s thinking to himself &#8220;look how great Gordon is.&#8221;  </p>
<p>Looking at the references to golden haired, it&#8217;s also interesting that Helen doesn&#8217;t react to the water running down her face at all.  If you take the water as very important in story, than a prominent &#8220;LACK OF REACTION&#8221; to it seems notable.  I&#8217;m kind of hinging on &#8220;conservation of narrative detail&#8221; here but all it seems too important to ignore.</p>
</div>
